      "diagnostic_reasoning": "The symptom of the engine randomly dying and then restarting after a 10-15 minute cool-down period strongly indicates an intermittent, heat-sensitive failure. This type of failure commonly affects the fuel, ignition, or sensor systems. \n\n1.  **Ignition System:** Components like the Ignition Control (IC) module, pick-up coil, or ignition coil are known to fail when hot and recover when cool, leading to a loss of spark (Source 27, 23, 33). The 'Hard Start' and 'Engine Cranks But Will Not Run' troubleshooting charts both include checking ignition voltage output/spark (Source 1, 15, 38).\n2.  **Fuel System:** An intermittent fuel pump or fuel pump relay failure, especially when hot, can cause a loss of fuel pressure, leading to stalling and preventing restart until the component cools (Source 1, 15, 38, 45). The FSM explicitly states that maintaining constant fuel pressure is critical and intermittent drops can cause driveability problems (Source 45).\n3.  **Sensor System:** An Engine Coolant Temperature (ECT) sensor that shifts in value when hot but remains within the ECM's acceptable operating window might not set a diagnostic code. This incorrect reading could cause the ECM to deliver an overly rich mixture, leading to rough running, stalling, and hard starting until the sensor cools and provides an accurate reading (Source 1, 15, 41). The FSM's 'Hard Start' procedure includes checking the ECT sensor (Source 1, 15).\n\nTo begin, we need to perform an On-Board Diagnostic (OBD) System Check to identify any stored trouble codes, as this is the first step in most troubleshooting procedures (Source 1, 15, 38). If no codes are present, we must then check for the presence of fuel and spark *when the engine is in its non-starting state* to isolate the failing subsystem.",

The fact that you hear the fuel pump running when the engine dies indicates that the ECM is receiving ignition reference pulses and is commanding the fuel pump ON (Source 1, 7, 11, 52). This points away from a complete loss of ignition signal to the ECM, but does not rule out a lack of fuel pressure or an ignition problem that prevents spark despite the pump running. The description of the problem is highly consistent with vapor lock, which is aggravated by heat and can cause the engine to shut off and be difficult to restart for 1-3 hours (Source 4). Other fuel delivery issues like a weak fuel pump, restricted lines, or a faulty pressure regulator could also cause intermittent fuel starvation, especially under load or when hot (Source 11, 29). Therefore, the most critical next step is to verify actual fuel pressure when the engine is in its non-starting state.       "mechanic_instructions": "You've confirmed low or foamy fluid. Fill the reservoir to the FULL COLD mark with the engine off and the drive unit straight back. Then, start the engine and run it at a fast idle (1000-1500 rpm), cycling the trim up and down several times to full travel in both directions. The FSM explicitly lists 'Warm engine carburetor percolation' and 'Vapor lock' as causes for 'Engine will not start after warm engine shut down' and 'Fuel boils out of float bowl when shut off and warm. This is a highly probable cause.\n\n2.  **Engine Coolant Temperature (ECT) Sensor Malfunction:** An ECT sensor that provides an inaccurate, excessively low temperature reading to the ECM when the engine is hot would cause the ECM to command an overly rich fuel mixture.       "mechanic_instructions": "A low coolant level is a direct cause of overheating. The most common reason for this after a coolant change or system repair is air trapped in the closed cooling system, or a new leak. You need to properly fill the system and purge any trapped air, then carefully inspect for leaks.\n\n1.  **Top off the coolant:** With the engine cool, remove the pressure cap from the heat exchanger. Add the recommended coolant solution until the level is full (to the bottom of the filler neck). (Source 1, Page 17; Source 2, Page 32)\n2.  **Purge air:** Start the engine and run it at idle. The front of the engine should be higher than the rear to help purge trapped air. (Source 31, Page 31). As the engine warms and the thermostat opens, continue to add coolant to the heat exchanger until the level is into the filler neck and begins to flow into the coolant recovery bottle plastic tubing. (Source 2, Page 32)\n3.  **Fill recovery bottle:** Remove the cap from the coolant recovery reservoir and fill to the 'FULL' mark. Lift the recovery bottle and plastic tubing above the heat exchanger filler neck to allow coolant to flow down and purge air. (Source 2, Page 32)\n4.  **Inspect for leaks:** With the engine still running at normal operating temperature, check all hose connections, fittings, and gaskets for visible leaks. Listen for any hissing sounds. (Source 2, Page 32; Source 49, Page 21)\n5.  **Observe recovery system:** Ensure the coolant recovery system is functioning. Normal operation involves approximately 1/2 pint (230 mL) of coolant overflowing into the recovery bottle during warm-up, and then drawing it back as the engine cools. Use a shop tachometer (error less than 5%) and a stopwatch for accuracy. An 8 U.S. qt. (7.6 L) or larger container is required for measuring output.\n\n1.
